' a revolver and fearing that he would
I shoot him. Crasshey dashed from Jhe hall
i and ran down O'Farrell street. Williams
J started after him and failing to overtake

The Red Cross Society has taken up the
matter of clothing the returning volun
teers, and will probably solve it In some
way within a day or two. It was thought
at one time yesterday the question had
been settled, and Mrs. W. B. Harrington
drove out to the Presidio to tell General
Summers that the department would fur
nish overcoats upon the receipt of the
men, the coats to be returned to the quar
j termaster when the men were mustered
I out. It seems some member of the so
! ciety gained that impression from Colonel
j Long, depot quartermaster, who, on be
i ing asked if he would furnish over
coats, replied that he had orders from
Washington to furnish all the warm
clothing necessary. This was taken to
mean that the clothing would be furnished
free to the men. Colonel Long also said
there had been a number of coats sent out
to the Presidio for the use of the troops,
but General Summers declared he had' not
seen them. Late in the evening Mrs. Har
rington admitted that perhaps there had
been a mistake, and the matter would
looked into further by the Red Cross bo
ciety. Â _. __
"I am beginning to believe," Mrs. Har
rington said last night "that the Govern
ment cannot furnish the overcoats to the
men unless by deducting the price from
their money, but we may hear more *
about it to-morrow. If the Government
cannot do it. then we must do it. *je .
must see that every man has all he
wants in the way of clothes, and then j
When he goes home he can turn back to |
us what we have loaned him and we can j
then let some other soldier be benefited. i
Colonel Summers has fixed the clothing i
matter to his own satisfaction by having ;
each captain see that the men he details j
to euard duty shall be provided with !
overcoats. That Is all he can do. he says. ;
unless he order each man to draw one, \
and this he does not contemplate, mere
are about three of four hundred over- j
coats on hand at the regimental head
auartersânot regulation army coats but ,
cheaper onesâ which are valued at less
than $5. These he will issue as fast as the
men wish to apply for them but so far
they have shown no Inclination to make
application. .'â¢â  _ .
The Government is now preparing to
ship cavalry horses by the thousands to
the Philippines and the commandant of
the Presidio Is making ready to receive :
them on their way across the ocean.
Three thousand horses and three hundred
mules have been purchased In the East,
and "they will be hurried out here as fast
as possible. The stables at the post will '
not accommodate more than three hun
dred horses, and the big corral at the
foot of the parade ground and behind the
cavalry stables will be only large enough
Ito accommodate the mules. It looks as
If the greater number of the horses will .
! have to be picketed out, as if they were
i already on the field. It is not yet known
i how many will come in the first install
' ment or how many will be kept at the
! post at one time, but it is expected that
the horses will be hurried on as rapidly
a. possible. "â -.- â 'â  'â¢
Lieutenant Colonel Maus, the new In
spector general of this department, paid
an official visit to the Presidio yesterday
and inspected all the camps and post
buildings. He found the camps clean and
well policed and the buildings in good con
dition. It was his first inspection since j
taking offlce. Â«Â»Â»_Â«_*

had been appointed a lieutenant In the I
provisional army now being formed by
the President. Lieutenant Tremalne has
been attached to the department head
quarters for a number of years and was
on the personal staff of General Shafter
during the Cuban campaign. He has not

maine was in the regular army before he

News has also been received that George
B. Sharon, son of Clarence Sharon, the
mining man and rancher, has been ap
pointed a second lieutenant in the pro
visional army. _; '.

CRUSHED UNDER TON OF ROCK.
SAUSALITO. July IS.â August Undo, a
workman employed in the quarry at Angel
Island, met with a horrible accident yes
terday while assisting in the loading of a

death to relieve him of his agony.
"Warring Doctors.
The war between the College of Phy
sicians and Surgeons and the Cooper .Col
lege and medical department of the Uni
versity over the division of wards in the
City and County Hospital was practically
ended at a meeting of the Board of Health
yesterday, when all the medical factions
were represented. The representatives of
the College of Physicians and Surgeons
decided to let Cooper and the medical de
partment of the University have flrst
choice of wards. By the present arrange
ment the College of Physicians and Sur
geons has three and one-half wards and
the other two colleges four wards each.

BLACK DRAUGHT
HER DEATH POTION
Continued from First Page.
Hotel Waldorf-Astoria in New York
City.
She was left penniless but in some
way, possibly realizing on her jewels,
she secured funds with which to return
to San Francisco and arrived here three
weeks ago. She went at once to Bailey
and pleaded to be taken back, but dur
ing her absence he had made other ar
rangements; another fair one was rul
ing the hearth at 110 Jones street.
A few nights later after a whirl
across the Ellis-street divide, which
was interrupted only as often as the
absinthe . could be frozen. Florence
broke in on the peace and quiet at the
Jones street apartments with an idea
of doing harm to hersuccessor. The dis
turbance she raised was of such ample
proportions that she was ordered out
by the landlady and Bailey received an
ultimatum not to let it happen again.
Then drink got the upper hand. Flor
ence became melancholy and brooded
over her troubles almost constantly. Her
prosperous past was dead to her: she
had gone her pace and in eight 'hort
years had found the finish. Between
brooding and drinking she worked her
self into another frenzy and a few days
ago again went to Bailey's stable. She
found him just about to drive out and
seizing the carriage whip from its
socket she used it on him without
mercy:
Then she went home and took car
bolic acid.
Prompt work saved her life, and on
Friday last she was able to leave her
rooms. She telephoned to one of her
companions, Lil Nelson, and her
mother, forsaking Senator Bettman for
the night. The three left for the cele
bration at the Chutes. Their night was
one of drunken revelry and abandoned
gayety. Florence was almost feverish
in her high spirits; the melancholy that
had clung to her was drowned in a cold
i green bath.
*
Between 3 and 4 o'clock in the morn
ing she left her mother and the Nelson
woman without warning and took the
first car she could catch into town. Ar
rived at Market street, she made her
way rapidly to Bailey's rooms, where
she raised another disturbance. Bailey
came out to her and begged her to be
quiet. He said he would get a carriage
and they would find other rooms, and
she promised to wait there until he had
done so.
But, either doubting Bailey's good
faith or laboring under another fit of
melancholy, she broke her promise and
hurried out to her home and stole qui
etly in. Without rousing any one she
made her way to her room, where for
days she had kept concealed a bottle of
laudanum.
Her determination to suicide had not
come in a moment. Since she had been
deserted in New York she had thought
of it, and as her melancholy increased
the idea became so fixed in her mind
that it was always with her. Her let
ters indicated it, as did her conversa
: tion. Life to her was so cheap as to be
scarcely worth the taking.
But she took the draught, and an
hour or so later her almost lifeless body
was found dressed as she had returned
from her night at the Chutes.
To hush the matter up was the first
matter to which the attention of Sena
tor Bettman and his mistress, the dead
girl's mother, was turned. If it be
came known that she had suicided the
whole disgraceful story of the riotous
life at the Devisadero-street house
would transpire at the inquest.
Bettman decided that it would be best
to have the body taken at. once to the
Columbarium and there cremated, but
here the dead girl's brother. Royal Par
ker of Fresno, stepped in. He had
stood the disgraceful state of affairs for
years, he said, but he would not stand
any such cold-blooded scheme as Bett
man proposed. His sister's body should
be buried with that of his father in
the cemetery at Stockton, and this
Bettman, although the head of the
house, was compelled to assent to.
He went to the proper people, how
ever, and making use of his entreaties
and political pull induced them to de
cide that Florence Parker had died of
I dilation of the heart and kindred
j troubles.
I She was buried yesterday at Stock
ton.

THE EXAMINER'S OVERCOATS.
THE CALL sincerely hopes that the Oregon
volunteers, who are among the 'hardiest soldiers
fhat were furnished by .the northern part of the
United States, may not attempt to protect themselves
from the winds and the fogs incident to the climate
of San Francisco by wearing the underclothing or the
overcoats furnished by the Examiner. So far as we
are advised we are unaware that any of these articles
has ever become visible to the naked eye. But we can
assure the volunteers that, if they should materialize
under atmospheric or other influences, they will turn
out to be things of shreds and patches, without
warmth and open to all the cold and driving mists
that occasionally float through the Golden Gate.
The Government, the press and the people are
united in deep appreciation of the valor, the endur
ance -and the intelligent patriotism displayed by our
volunteer forces in the Philippines. The bright citi
zen soldiery of Oregon have deserved and have re
ceived conspicuous honors. But they did not require
the insolent pretensions of the Examiner to secure
for themselves ordinary protection against the
weather, for which there is the most ample provision
in the army regulations and in the Government stores
in San Francisco. Each of these representatives of
Northwestern devotion to our national institutions is
entitled to exercise his option by taking an overcoat
or the sum of $11 69, which represents its cost.
Many of them have preferred to save the money, and
have made other arrangements for their own com
fort and for their own health entirely satisfactory to
themselves. There is and there has been no red tape
that in any way obstructed or interfered with them.
No action of any kind by the President of the United
States or by General Miles, or by any department or
officer of the Government, outside of the usual rou
tine, was necessary to facilitate them in any precau
tionary measures they may have desired to take.
~- *â â¢ â¢'"..'-'_ 1 _1 T) â __
Th.c Examiner is well understood on the Pacific
Coast, and in its vulgar assumption of some mys
terious power it could exercise for the benefit of the
Oregon volunteers it has simply added one more il
lustration to the volume it has already produced of its
ineradicable delusions. Its entire stock in trade ap
parently consists of virulent egotism, displayed in
hysterical type. Each successive incident in its career
has been a phase of paresis. It offered a reward of
fifty thousand dollars for the discovery of the crim
inal who destroyed the Maine. It appointed a com
mission of its own to regulate the official representa
tives of the Government. It adopted a "national
policy." the essential point of which was the incor
poration of the Philippines as American territory and
of the Filipinos as our fellow citizens. It then pro
claimed an "internal policy," which still parades its
glaring absurdities at the head of its editorial page. It
has produced fabricated interviews with some of the
potentates of the earth, having both terrestrial and
spiritual functions, in which it pretended to deal with
them on the footing of an independent power.
The latest aspect of this extraordinary lunacy or
idiocy is the publication of editorial letters addressed
to President McKinley, usually signed by the pro
prietor of the Examiner, through which unread in
structions are conveyed to the Government. In this
category the absurd telegram about the overcoats is
included. The performance was interesting on ac
count of its grotesque absurdity. In the daily walks
of life examples of vanity or egotism are not uncom
mon, but it is rarely that those diseases are the only
characteristic features of a daily newspaper.
The Oregonian volunteers will not shiver in this
balmy climate, where popular enthusiasm alone would
almost keep them warm, but they would certainly find
the Examiner overcoats in the nature of wet blankets.
The Philadelphia Press, an acknowledged mouth
piece of the administration, says editorially that if the
allegations of the correspondents at \Janila are sus
tained by investigation Major General Otis will be
removed. The General would better begin packing
his trunks.
Friends of Superior Judge Murasky were some
what alarmed last week by the report that he had
Lung trouble. Their minds were considerably
eased when they discovered that he was only suffer
ing: from a pun perpetrated in a navy divorce case.
DYER LOYALTY.
CAPTAIN N. MAYO DYER has landed in Bos
ton and immediately indulged in an expression
of opinion and belief, to the effect that all anti
imperialists are traitors, and that the time has arrived
by his watch to suppress them. The captain lays
down the law to the effect that citizens who do not
support a national administration are disloyal. He
plunges into print up to where he parts his name, and
lectures the American people, who pay his salary, as
if they were Filipinos adjudged incapable of self
government.
His idea of our institutions has the trent of sim
plicity. The people owe loyalty to the President an.l
must support whatever the President is doing. When
the President says that acquisition of territory by con
quest is criminal aggression and opposed to our na
tional morality the people must say "amen!" When
the President changes, and spends eight hundred
thousand dollars a day in an attempt to acquire the
Philippines by conquest, and the facts are kept from
the people whose money is being spent in that ad
| venture, Captain Dyer insists that any opposing citi
| zen is a traitor, and this horse marine desires to sup
press him.
The logic of Captain Dyer is a twin to that of Dip
lomat John Barrett. According to it, when the Presi
dent said that conquest of territory was criminal ag
gression all the imperialists were traitors and should
have been suppressed, and the anti-imperialists were
loyalists. Then the President suddenly said "Wig
wag" and "Thumbs down," and the imperialists,
purged of treason, made faces at the disloyal anti
imperialists.
Captain Dyer has perhaps grown rusty on salt water
in respect to the nature of our Government and the
citizen's duty toward it. When he entered the navy
'he took an oath to support the constitution of the
United States and defend its principles and to obey
the orders of his superiors. The President of the
United States, commander-in-chief of the army and
navy, is Captain Dyer's superior officer, whose orders
he must obey. But the captain seems to think that he
is the superior of the plain tax-paying citizens, and
that they must obey him as he obeys the President.
This Nickel Neptune is in error. He may be sur
prised to learn that the American people do not take
an oath of loyalty and obedience to him or the Presi
dent. He seems to fancy that at some stage in the
proceedings every citizen takes an oath something like
this:
I do solemnly swear that I will true allegiance
and obedience render to Captain X. Mayo Dyer, and
in all matters relating to the policy of the National
Government, forsaking all others, will cleave to his
opinion until he changes it.
But this seadog is on the wrong trail. The only
oath of allegiance taken by the people cf this country
is to support the constitution. When they hear a
major general in our army declare that the constitu
tion is obsolete, and that imperialism has no use for it
nor the Declaration of Independence, they naturally
protest against a policy which requires repudiation of
every foundation principle of our Government. For
making and publishing this protest Captain Dyer
would have them punished as traitors, not to the con
stitution, nor to the Government, but to the Presi
dent!
Now, while it is true that a pious person over in
Oakland has announced that President McKinley de
rives his authority not from the people but from God
direct, and proves by texts running from early in
Genesis to late in Revelation, that the wholesale
slaughter of the Filipinos was being divinely planned
while the universe was chewing prehistoric peanuts at
the first matinee, and when the morning stars sang
together, and that Major McKinley was really nomi- j
nated in a celestial caucus and sprung on the Ameri- j
can people as a miracle, there be those who are ;
skeptical. The Kaiser and Captain Dyer are suffi- j
cient for talking partners in the scheme for running
the world by divine right.
The captain should be informed that our institutions
are like water. You may draw your hand edgewise
through it and there seems to be no resistance, but
hit it a flat blow and it is as unyielding as steel. He
and a few other military and civil fools are playing
with the principles of our Government. They seem
to be having all the fun, but when they get ready to |
deal a blow the unyielding work of the fathers will j
stand, and those who would destroy it will be shat

tered.
CROP PROSPECTS FOR THE YEAR.
rROM reports contained in the Crop Circular for j
June, issued by the United States Department of I
Agriculture, it appears there is to be a shortage
in the wheat output of the world and in the apple and
the peach crop of the United States. The outlook for
good prices for such farm products is therefore ex
cellent.
In regard to the apple crop the circular says: "In
the thirteen States having 3,000,000 or more apple
trees in bearing at the last census the condition on
June 1, as compared with the average June condition
for the last fifteen years, was as follows: New York.
1 above; Pennsylvania, 6 below; Michigan, 14 below;
Missouri, 2 above; Illinois, 8 above; Indiana, 5 above;
Kansas, 4 above; Kentucky, no difference; Tennes
see, 2 above; Virginia, 3 below; North Carolina, 4
below; lowa, ii below, and Maine, 31 below."
The peach crop, it is announced, will probably
come as near being a total failure as it ever will come
in a country of such vast extent and such varied cli
matic conditions as the United States. With the ex- \
ception of California, where the conditions indicate
from 75 to 95 per cent of a full crop, there is not a
State that has the promise of so much as two-thirds
of a normal crop; few look for even a half crop, and |
in many important peach-growing States there will be I
practically no crop whatever. In the last-named
category must be placed Pennsylvania, Delaware,
Georgia, Ohio, Illinois, Indiana, Missouri, Alabama,
Tennessee 'and Kentucky!
Preliminary returns of the wheat crop for this
country show that of twenty States having 10,000
acres or more devoted to that product six report an
increase of acreage over that of last year amounting
in all to 245,000 acres, while fourteen report a de- I
crease aggregating 715,000 acres, so that there will be
a net decrease for the whole country of about 470,000
acres. The average condition of the crop, moreover,
is reported as but 91.4, as compared to an average of
100.0 at the corresponding season of last year.
Reports from foreign countries indicate a decrease
elsewhere in the crop. It is stated the Russian re
ports represent winter grain crops, both wheat and
rye, as almost destroyed by drought, by which the
spring grains also had been severely injured and
would soon be beyond help unless relieved by rain.
It is evident that the crop of bread grain for the em
pire as a whole cannot be a good one. notwithstanding
that for a few localities which have been heard from
the reports are generally favorable. It has even been
suggested that the crop may not exceed that of 1897.
A somewhat hotter showing is made for the crop of
France, which, next to Russia, is the largest and most
important wheat-producing country in Europe, but i
even there the yield is not expected to equal that cf |
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL,, WEDNESDAY,- JULY 19, 1899.
last year. An official estimate, issued May 10, reports
that while the area under wheat is about the same as
in the preceding season, the condition is 5 per cent
lower, which indicates the crop will be about 20,000,
--000 bushels less than that of 1898.
A British commercial estimate cited by the circular
tentatively puts the world's wheat crop, of 1899 at
2,504,000,000 bushels, against 2,748,000,000 bushels in
1898, a reduction of 244,000,000 bushels, or nearly 8.9
per cent. Another estimate makes a reduction of
352,000,000 bushels.
These estimates are of course largely matters of
speculation. It is as yet too early to determine with
anything like accuracy what the outcome will be.
They are based, however, on the best information the
Government can obtain, and are, therefore, valuable
as indications of the probabilities of the crops for the
year. The outlook as shown by them is encouraging
for California, and our farmers and orchardists may
reasonably expect a season of more than ordinary
prosperity, with good prices for both grain and fruit.
WHATEVER questions of law may be here
after raised in respect to the municipal assess
ment for the present fiscal year, the action of
the Board of Supervisors on Monday last, and espe
cially of its Rei Ã¼blican members, is a new and an
honorable precedent in the history of San Francisco.
The astute lawyers and manipulators who represent
the corporations may have cards up their sleeves, but
that will not alter the fact that, for once, a pledge has
been kept and at least an approach toward equality
in assessments secured.
The Call does not dispute the proposition that, in
the distribution of credit for this desirable result,
there are Democrats who are entitled to their legiti
mate share. But the brag of the Examiner and its
pretended claim that the substantial approach toward
justice that the completed equalization discloses is
due to the action of Assessor Dodge is unwarrantable.
While it is true that the Assessor raised the valuations
of the personal property of the Market Street Rail
way Company and of other corporations, it is equally
true that he carefully and unjustly preserved the dis
proportion between corporations and private citizens
that had so long existed. He was compelled to ad
mit that, in the case of ordinary property-owners, he
had adopted a percentage of from 75 to 90, while cor
porations had been appraised at from 50 to 60 per cent
of the market value of their bonds, franchises and
other property, and the only reason he could assign
for this discrimination was his fear that he might be
suspected of "cinching" propensities.
The Assessor's argument was as fallacious as the
boasting of the Examiner was absurd. The only true
rule is that of substantial equality before the law.
Corporations and individuals, in the theory of taxa
tion, are upon a level. Neither are entitled to the
slightest advantage over the other. Under any just
system of assessment, the value of all property, with
out reference to its ownership, should be fairly ascer
tained and an undiscriminating rate of percentage
adopted. But, if this precision is unattainable and if
some inequalities are inevitable, then aggregated and
swollen capital and not private citizens should be sub
jected to the highest rate.
The Examiner demands credit for its efforts to
elect Assessor Dodge. But it was owing to this par
ticular officer that the greatest inequalities were intro
duced into the assessment, and had not the Board of
Supervisors, so far as was practicable under existing
conditions, rectified this inequality and by its method
of equalization kept the entire valuation of property
within reasonable limits, not only would small prop
erty-owners have been sacrificed but the municipality
would have been placed at the mercy of the State.
The essential point to be observed is that a large
quantity of property that in former years has fraudu
lently escaped from any contribution to municipal ex- j
penditure has now been forced out of its seclusion,
and that while it is still relieved from a full share of
the public burthens, a long stride has been made
toward an honest interpretation of the constitutional
and statutory provisions on the subject of taxation. j
The corrupt politicians who have been used by the
Huntington monopoly and by other kindred organi- j
zations to cheat the average citizen and to buy ex
emption for their employers for once have failed.
Some preparations at least have been made for a com
plete trial of the merits of the new charter.
The year has marked the commencement of a new
era in municipal and in State politics, and in the prac
tical administration of the various departments of
government. Republican integrity in the Legislature
was^oo much for Dan Burns. Republican and Demo
cratic integrity in the Board of Supervisors overcame
all the machinations of the plotters of the Market
â¢Street Railway Company. Now there has been an
approximation toward justice in local taxation, for j
which also the Board of Supervisors, and not the Ex- I
aminer or Assessor Dodge, are to be commended.
The prospects of the Republican party in the ap- |
proaching campaign have been greatly brightened by â 
the events of the past few weeks.
AN EXPERIMENT WORTH TRYING.
AN association known as "The German House
wives' Society" has presented to the Municipal
Assembly of New York City a petition praying
that the city government undertake an official inves
tigation of the conditions of domestic service,' with the
object, as stated in the petition, of bringing about a
better understanding between mistress and maid.
The movement can hardly be called another step in
the direction of paternalism, for the paternal head of
a family is not charged with the solution of servant
girl problems. It is a tendency toward maternalism
in municipal affairs, and, that being something new in
civic development, the experiment is wortn trying â
at least it is worth trying on the other side of the
continent.
Something is needed just now to add to the gayety
of nations. Our comedies are dull, our farces are
coarse, our funny men have gone stale, and all the
humorists have climbed the chestnut tree. There
fore why not turn the Municipal Assembly of New
York loose to investigate the relations,* the duties and
the troubles that exist between the housewife and the
servant girl? The scheme is quite feasible and the re
sults are sure to be entertaining.
It would require no great amount of imagination
to construct a good plot for a comic opera out of
the proceedings of a committee of city authorities
gravely interfering between mistress and maid. It is
true the petition for such interference has come from
housewives, but it is safe to say that if it be under
taken the housewives as well as the servant girls will
turn up their noses at the masculine presumption, and
perhaps have hysterics if it be persisted in.
Nevertheless we repeat the thing is worth trying,
and the Tammany Aldermen are the very men to be
appointed to try it. The contest between- the tiger
and the woman is just the thing we need to enliven
the dullness of the summer season.

ASSESSMENTS EQUALIZED.
HOTEL THAT TRAVELS ON WHEELS.
Folds Up When Not In Use and Handled Like an Ordinary
Baggage Car.
ONE of the most remarkable of the recent inventions upon
which the Patent Office authorities have been called to
pass judgment in a traveling hotel, the designer of which
thinks he ha* filled a long-felt want by constructing a por
table habitation for traveling circuses or camping parties who
do not wish to be at the mercy of local hotel keepers.
The hotel on wheels is made, of aluminum, and is so con
structed as to fold up like a railroad car, and to appear, when
closed, to be an ordinary baggage car. When the stopping
place is reached and the travelers wish to camp for the night
the sides of the car are let down by means of a series of levers.
The inventor has divided his traveling hotel into, compart
ments, so that the party, if it be a large one, can be separated
LET PATRIOTS BE CONSISTENT.
Editor of The Callâ Sir: As an American of Irish birth I beg to express my
warmest sympathy with the views set forth in your editorial in The Call of
July 13 entitled "A Holy War."
Consistency, truth and justice permeate every paragraph of your excellent
article, which deals honestly and fearlessly with a vital questionâ a new "im
pending conflict" â the affairs of our country.
Nearly forty years ago a true Irishman, who subsequently won the laurels
of a Union general (Charles G. Halpine, known in letters as "Private Myles
O'Reilly"), with sad resentment from his patriotic heart penned the lines:
"Tear down the flaunting lie,
"' Half-mast the starry flag;
Insult no sunny sky
With hate's polluted rag."
The sentiments thus expressed (in the New York Tribune) were regarded
as disloyal, traitorous, etc., by many well meaning but misguided people of
that period. But to the millions who believed that human beings with immor
tal souls could not and should not be sold as chattels at the auctioneer's
block under the shadow of a flag claimed to be "the flag of the free," the ut
terance of the poet bore an entirely different significance.
The Call to-day, to its honor be it said, substantially enunciates the same
principle which the poet proclaimed to the confusion and shame of certain
demagogic politicians and orators of that period.
To peoples as well as individuals the truth is not always sweet and flatter
ing. On the contrary, it is often bitter and humiliating. In our heart of
hearts to-day we revere the loyalty, courage and unswerving patriotism of our
"gallant soldiers and sailors, whose matchless bravery on land and sea has
evoked the admiration of the world. But the glorious achievements of sol
diers and sailorsâ solemnly sworn to obedience â and the merits of the cause in
which a higher power uses and utilizes their prowess and daring are questions
entirely separate and distinct. What are the lessons of history to some of our
fellow-citizens who at the present time are essaying to mold public opinion
upon a subject which all thoughtful men must regard as a crucial test of our
nation's claims and standing as the friend and protector of liberty and human
rights? Alas, how applicable are the words of the illustrious Madame Roland:
"Oh, Liberty! Liberty! How many crimes are committed in thy name." '.'."fy
If some of my Irish friends and fellow countrymen who delight to indulge
in flights of vehement oratory upon the wrongs and sufferings of the land that
bore us could only see in the unquestionably brave and still unconquered Fili
pinos a picture of their own ancestors, likewise brave and liberty-loving, who
in the bogs and behind the sweet smelling hawthorne hedges of Ireland fought
to the death the troopers of Cromwell and the "bloody year" of '98, they, the
aforesaid friends and fellow countrymen, would for shame's sake change the
tone of their orations. Nothing seems worse in the speech, of a public man
than glaring inconsistency and an utter disregard of principle.
Shakespeare must have known of such a style of oratory when he wrote:
"What damned error, but some sober brow
Will bless it, and approve it with a text,
Hiding the grossness with fair ornament?"
The Rev. Mr. Hensons, the Whitelaw Relds and also certain" gentlemen
nearer home should carefully examine their "brows" and determine whethe:
or not they are of the "sober" type described by the immortal bard of Avon. :
San Francisco, July IS, 1599. . JUSTICE.

the Herald building, Xew York City, and
will come by road to San Francisco, a
distance by shortest railroad of 3250 miles,

terested in its manufacture believe that

the Electric Vehicle Company of New
i York has placed orders for the autos to

EXTRAVAGANT
"BLUE BEARD"
AT THE TIVOLI
No hours of deep thought exhausted the
brain of the author when the libretto of
"Blue Beard" was building. In a merry
mood he sat him down and wrote â
and occasionally they rhymed. Hence it
ls not the beautifulest, most moving or
finest writ we have seen in a twelvemonth,
and withouten a bit of right good cook
ing by the cast it might breed Indigestion.
Old English comes to the pen by right of
environment, for the scenic artistâ
wizard! â has accomplished the feat of
showing us, under the minarets of Tur
key, the ancestraf piles of our British
ancestors in close and grotesque perspect
ive!' It is just possible that even ex
travaganzas can be a shade too extrava
gant. When there are so many clever
ways of being funny to choose from why
offend the eye? The third set is well
treated if a little startling. It is as clear,
flat, dry and unatmospheric as a picture
by Gerome, and makes a flne background
for the incongruous, inharmonious figures
that move across the stage. And they are
inharmonious. No color scheme seems to
actuate the grouping and grace and
rhythm lose their charm in the clash that
results. Even the lights are badly chosen.
At times they give one a sea-sick feeling.
All this ugliness is made the medium for
some really beautiful soli and choruses
and some exceedingly clever dancing and
specialty work. Miss Walker's song from
the balcony was so beautifully sung that
no one would suspect the metallic quality
that later work discloses in her voice.
She seems, however, a singer of experi
ence and an actress of no mean ability.
The couleurature in her voice, if of the
unfinished order, is agreeable and will
please the majority. Miss Myers sang
her song of "Jack" to spontaneous ap
plause and a well-deserved encore. Mr.
Pruette is "much less than kind" to him
self when he accepts the part of Sister
i Anne, but the audience finds him funny
in the various sections of the building, occupying, if they wish,
apartments en suite or a single room. The rooms are to be fitted
up in the modern style, with all conveniences, including elec
tric light, electric bells, electric cooking apparatus, and the
inventor is even working on a system that he thinks will en
able the occupants of the hotel to reach the upper story by
means of an electric eievator.
The furniture designed for the car is, of course, mostly or
the folding variety, and can be stowed away in a very small
compass when not in use. When the stopping place is reached
the car is to be detached from the train and taken to a con
venient siding, where there Is ample room for the spreading
of the sides and the elevation of the roof that form the hotel.
and perhaps this compensates for the sac
rifice. Mr. Stevens has ever a wav of
being amusing without effort and as Hans
Fritz Ibrahim the quality stands him in
good stead. So stupid are the lines given
him to speak that he gets his biggest
laughs by belaboring his lunch counter
victims (rather clever types by the wav)
with French loaves and custard pie's.
Given the right lines no one can make a
point better than Mr. Stevens. Why is
he not given them? Will not some one
with a trick for rhyme lend his intelli
gence to a worthy libretto? For the man
who does, nothing in this world will be
too good, and when he dies no cemetery
will be worthy of his remains.
Of the two dances "The Man and the
Maid" is by far the prettier and would
be better named "The Butterfly and the
Beetle."
Now a word as to coster songs. They
are a realm into which few may venture
with impunity and it does not subtract
from the value of the cast to say they
are not found on the Tivoli stage. Peo
ple who sing coster songs well usually do
nothing else. Mr. Branson was the only
one who caught their spirit and even in
his case another choice would have been
wiser. The extravaganza, thanks to the
gift of enthusiasm, is playing to large
houses and much applause, but will be
withdrawn at the close of the week to
give place to "Wang." in which Mr.
Stevens does excellent work.

HORTICULTURISTS AND VITI
CULTURISTS ENTHUSED.
W. H. McNeil and L. A. Emlay, Men
cf Wide Experience. Will Col
lect and Arrange the Im
portant Displays.
The California Paris Exposition Com
mission is doing its utmost to arouse
farmers ami yineyardists to a full appre
ciation of the vast advertising possibili
ties . of the coming Paris Exposition.
When Californians realize that half the
space allotted to the whole United States
for a horticultural display has been given
to California and over two-thirds of the
viticultural space has also been assigned
to the Golden State they will probably
make a concerted effort to send the best
that the State produces to France and
see that not an inch of space is wasted.
The commission has established perma
nent headquarters in the Occidental Ho
iel. Circulars have been sent^to ill the
prominent growers in the State, and
while many are arranging for space, the
response is not what it might be.
The collection and installation of bev
erages is a. part of the agricultural ex
hibit of this country. which by law is to
be prepared by the Secretary of Agricui-.
ture. Commissioner General Peck and the
Secretary of Agriculture have -mated
C. R. E)odge as a director of agricul
ture, and Dr. H. W. Wiley, chief chemist
of the Department of Agriculture-, has j
been appointed expert in cha-rse of the ;
collection of the exhibit. Dr. Wiley has
arranged for the collection of the Cali
fornia, display by the California Paris Ex
position Commission: and this commis
sion has appointed W. H. McNeil its '
agent to represent Dr. Wiley.
This work is thoroughly familiar to Mr.
McNeil. For many years he had charge
of the exhibit in th*r rooms of the Viti
cultural Commissioners and was selected
by the California World's Fair Commis- I
tion to take charge of the viticultural fea- i
tures of the California display at the Co
iumbian Exposition. The importance of j
the concessions at the coining Paris Ex
position is concisely stated in one of the]
circulars as follows:
Now is the time for the viticultur
:.-: o: California to make a record.
France having been the home of the
wine industry for ages, comparisons
al! over the world are made by the
French standard, therefore at the
Paris Exposition of 1900 is the oppor
tunity for California wines to get the
! world's recognition. The wines of
California have been assigned suffi
cient space by the United States Com
missioners to have them represented
as never before in any foreign coun
try. Every time California has made
a viticultural exhibit the State has
made a lasting impression and gained
increased honor and reputation.
The horticultural and agricultural dis
plays will be in the hands of L. A. Em
lay, who is well acquainted with the pro
ducers of the State. In I>C<3 he was se
lected as flrst assistant to Mr. Wells, then
superintendent of the agricultural and
horticultural departments of the World's
Fair at Chicago. When Mr. Weils re
signed. Mr. Emlay was placed in harge
of that department in both the national
and State buildings, and performed his
duties most creditabiv.

Suit in Bankruptcy.
The Sorosis Fruit Company filed a suit
yesterday against C. B. Bills, as trustee
of the estate of E. B. Howard, bankrupt,
for a restraining order enjoining the trus
tee from proceeding with the bankrupt
matter in the United States District
Court until an accounting shall have been
had between the plaintiff and Howard.

FOOD INSPECTORS AT WORK.
- â â 
A Filthy Candy Factory and Delicat
ressen Store on Third Street
Closed.
Chief Food Inspector Devoto and Depu- j
ty Lahaney yesterday afternoon closed
the pork and delicatessen store of Henry
Voll at 121 Third street and the candy
factory owned hy <;. Manetara at 128
Third street. Both places were being
operated under filthy and unsanitary con
ditions. In the cellar under the candy
factory a ceVpQol has collected from the
seepage of defective sewer pipes. Barrels
Of swill and other rubbish are scattered
around promiscuously and in the foul and
tainted air ice cream ha?* been daily man- !
ufactured for months. The first floor of
the huildlng is in about the same condi
tion as th*' cellar. Manetara's sleeping
room opens right off the room In which
he manufactures candy, and the whole
place is filled with a sickening odor from
the cellar.
Similar conditions prevailed in Voll's es
tablishment. His cellar is filled with open
barrels In which meat is set to corn.
There Is a floor of rotten boards and an
open grating, allows the dirt of the street
to filter down into the barrels with every
gust of wind. In his working room above I
where sausage and lard are manufactured !
conditions fully as bad if not worse pre
vail. The place looks as though it has
never seen broom or scrubbing brush. TnÂ«_
owners of the premises were notified to
have the leaking sewer pipes mended and
a concrete floor must be laid in Voll's
cellar before he can resume work. His
workshop must also be cleaned and ven
tilated.

DISCOURAGING
NEWS FROM THE
WHALING FLEET
Only Four Whales
Have Been Taken.
â * â
DEATH OF CAPTAIN BUCKLEY
â¢
INSANE WHEN HE ACCIDENT
ALLY SHOT HIMSELF.
Captain Balch of the John A. Briggs
Narrowly Escapes Meeting
Death by Drowning in
Delagoa Bay.
â¦
The steamer Portland, which arrived
from St. Michael yesterday, brought down
the remains of E. Towhill, a young San
Franciscan, who sought a fortune and
found death. He left here two years ago
and died from typhoid fever in Dawson
last February. A. L. Tracy, who returned
on the steamer, is in charge of the body.
The Portland brought considerable news
from the whaling fleet. So far it has been
a bad year, as very few whales have been
seen. The Narwhal, xielvedere, Bowhead
(the Norwegian steamer purchased and
commanded by Capta.n Cook) and Jean
ette have each taken one whale. All the
others are clean. Besides these, the Jean
ette had 7000 pounds of trade bone, so her
captain has no kick coming.
The death of Captain Buckley of the
steam whaler William Baylies was a very
sad affair. He had been under a severe
mental strain for several weeks and his
mind was somewhat unhinged. He could
not sleep at night and became subject to
hallucinations. He thought his crew was
talking about him behind his back and
sometimes he heard voices in the air.
From i to 9 a. m. of May 24 he was in
the crow's nest watching for a whale.
When he descended to the deck he said he
could hear a band playing In the air.
The William Baylies was fast to the ice
near Diomede Island when the accident
happened. At 8 p. m. on May 24 Captain
Buckley and Mate Cuttle were sitting in
the cabin, when the captain started and
said some one was trying to force his way
in, and ordered the mate to lock the door.
The mate had nearly all the keys In the
ship on a ring, as he had locked up a,
the guns and dangerous things for fear
Buckley might do himself some harm.
To humor tne captain, Cuttle took the
ring with the keys out of his pocket and
tried to find one that would fit the cabin
door. He failed and went to look for a
key that would fit, leaving the ring on
the cabin table. This was the captain's
opportunity. lie was conlident some one
was entering the cabin to kill him, s<>.
seizing the keys, he lilted one to the lock
on the lazarette and opening the batch
lowered himself down.
The letter that brought the particulars
down then goes on to say: "Captain
Buckley was clean out of his mind by
this time. He still thought he was bein?
followed, and. seeing a gun behind some
stores, he got down on his belly and'
grasping it by the muzzle pulled 11 to
ward him. The gun went off and blew
the top of his head away. His right hand
was ail powder-stained through being so
close to the muzzle. It was a gross piece
of. carelessness on the part of whoever
put that gun away loaded We buried
Captain Buckley on St. Lawrence island
ana Chief Officer Cuttle will take the
steamer north. The. other officers went
up a step and Charley Boyle has been
made fourth mate and Ike Smith a boat-
Fteerer. We are now bound for the Arctic
and 1 hope we will make a good catch."
Captain Balch, the well-known master
of the American ship John A. Briggs. ha I
a very narrow escape from losing his life
in Delagoa Bay last month. As It was
he lost all his money, the ships regis!
and all her papers, and is likely to have
some trouble at his next port of call in
consequence.
The John A. Briggs went from here to
Chemainus last December and there
loaded 1,483,216 feet of lumber for Delagoa
Bay. Captain Balch discharged his cargo
after considerable delay and then cleared
his ship for Sydney. N. S. W. At the lat
ter port she is to load coal for Honolulu
and from Honolulu will bring sugar to
San Francisco. ; y . 7
After clearing his ship Captain Balch
fot into his boat and started to gr aboard,
'our of his sailors were rowing, but aa
it was blowing very hard they did not
make a great deal of headway. Suddenly
a squall struck the boat and over she
went. It was dark, but by good luck the
captain and four men succeeded In get
ting on the bottom of the boat and then :
for an hour they hung on. chilli to the
bone; and thinking each moment would
be their last. Just when they had given !
up all hope the boat from a Norwegian
bark came along and picked them up. ,
Uncle Sam is going to take another Pa- j
cific Mail steamer for a transport. As
soon as the City of Sydney arrives from
Panama and way ports she will be turned !
over to the quartermaster's department 1
and will be fitted out for a trip to Ma
nila. The San Bias, now in port, will bo j
held here and will take up the Sydney's ;
Panama run.
The transport Newport was moved to
the Mall dock yesterday and will be fitted
out and loaded there for her next voyage
to Manila. " _, "
The San Juan sailed yesterday for Pan
ama and way ports. She took away fif
teen cabin passengers and considerable
freight. Captain Tilton. late chief officer
of the Newport, was a passenger. He I
goes to Panama to relieve Captain Buck
man, who comes to San Francisco on a
vacation. â¢ - â¢-

and she is now as good as new.

PASSED A FICTITIOUS CHECK.
John Hunt, a farmer from Santa Cruz.:
was arrested yesterday by Policeman Ker
rison and booked at the City Prison on a
charge of passing a fictitious check. Be- \
fore being taken to the prison he was es
corted to the French Consul's office, and
the Consul said he had done the same j
thing before.
Hunt had been living at the Xew Europe
Hotel. Pacific and Sanson c streets, and
yesterday morning when the landlord; J. j
Desbartas, demanded payment for his
board and lodging he pave him a check :
for $100 on the Crocker-Woolworth Bank, j
The check was taken to the hank to be
cashed; but there was no account.

IMPORTS OF SPECIE.
Imports of specie at this port during the first
Six months of the year were $10,844,797, against
$15 204 536 for the same time in ISSS. as follows:
Gold bullion. $355,433; gold in, $9,385,026; silver
bullion. $803,680; silver coin. $260,168. The lead
ing sources of these imports were: Australia,
$8,770,163; Mexico. $1,296,525.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure ha* remained nearly stationary
over the country west of the Rocky Mountains.
Along "the coast from San Luis Obispo to the]
mouth of the Columbia River there has been
but little change In temperature. In the In- |
terior there has been a rise of from 2 to 6 de
grees. This rise added to existing warm
weather has resulted In very high temperatures j
in the great vail* of California. .Through- i
out the Saji Joaquin and Sacramento valleys j
the temperature exceeded 100 degrees and in !
tome localities res ed as high as 110 degrees.
In th.c vicinity of San Francisco there is a j
horizontal difference of temperature of 48 de- '
grees In about fifty miles. At sea level near I
Sf.n Francisco the temperature is 52 degrees.
and at an elevation of half a mile 92 degrees i
at the same hour
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight July 19, 1599:
Northern Californiaâ Fair Wednesday, not
quite so warm In the interior, cooler near
Sacramento; northerly winds inland, brisk
westerly winds on the coast, with dense fog
at sea level
Southern Californiaâ Fair Wednesday; con
tinued warm weather with fog along the
coast in the morning; light westerly winds.
Nevada Fair Wednesday; continued warm
weather
Utahâ Fair Wednesday; continued warm
weather
Arizona â Cloudy Wednesday; warmer in the
extreme southwest.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Wednesday;
warmer ln the forenoon, dense fog in tne
morning; brisk westerly winds.
ALEXANDER G. McADIE.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. July 18.â stock market con
tinued dull and neglected, almost to the point
of stagnation to-day. Prices drifted quite aim
lessly up and down except in a few stocks
which were influenced by individual causes and
net changes for the most part were very small.
The opening showed some slight animation and
higher prices in sympathy with London, which
had advanced Americans before the opening
here and seemed to take the more cheerful view
of the general outlook. The money market
there was reported rather easier in tone, but
the Bank of England continued to do prac
tically all of the money lending at a rate
well above its official minumum rate of dis
count, and sterling exchange at Continental
centers showed additional sharp advances. In
New York, money was rather easier In tone,
but the rate still continued above those at
some other domestic centers, so That the sterl
ing exchange market, though hard, was not
quotably higher. It is intimated that money
rates may be held up in New York to prevent
an outflow of gold and the additional sugges
tion is heard that Chicago may supply London
with some gold on direct movement. There are
increasing offerings of Chicago funds in New-
York. Statements of railroad earnings con
tinue to break previous records, St. Paul lead
ing to-day on returns for its second July, with
an increase of f 231.532 over last year. To-day's
recovery in Brooklyn Transit amounted to two
points net and in Metropolitan Street Railway
to Is. withstanding the favorable inci
dents, the local stock market showed no dis
position to follow London's early lead and the
opening gains were soon lost. The only move
ments of any Importance were in Isolated
stocks, mostly industrials. Sugar and Manhat
tan showed early depression which was not
entirely overcome in the case of the latter,
though the former shows a small net gain.
American Smelting rose at one time 2*4 and
General Electric Is. but both reacted a. point.
New York Air Brake rose 6 points and then
dropped 11. The Tobacco stocks came into late
demand and gained from 1$ to 2s. the latter
Continental Tobacco common. Rubber pre
ferred, leather preferred, Tennessee Coal, Tin
Plate, Federal Steel, People's Gas and a few
other stocks had periods of Strength. New
York Central more than recovered yesterday's
decline and some firmness was manifest
among the Grangers. Pacifies and Trunk
lines, but .the coalers, Southwestern and
Southern stocks showed a slightly yielding dis
position. The whole day's business footed up
only a little over a quarter million shares and
Brooklyn Transit. Sugar and Manhattan ab
sorbed a large proportion of this.
The bond market was also very dull and price
changes were small and mixed as in stocks.
United States 3s coupon declined 'â (, and the
old 4s registered $, while the old 4s coupon ad
vanced $ In the bid price.

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK. July is. The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says: ;
| The feeling regarding the Transvaal being more
', hopeful to-day the tone of the stock market j
j was brighter, but there was , no increase of j
I business. Americans were dull until New York j
I began buying. Later they eased off. closing be- j
I low the opening. Changes In balances were :
I fractional and irregular. Money was easier ;
I and some balances were unemployed. The â 
â¢ bank did a smaller discount business and only
| a trifling amount of loans were repaid. Silver ,
. closed weak on American selling.

DRIED FRUITS, NUTS AND RAISINS.
After a long season of neglect Prunes are
waking up and business in them is decidedly
better Old crop lots are being taken up and
quotations for some sizes of the new crop have
already been established. There is an active
demand from Europe at 6'ic for 40-50' c, sftc for
50-60's and 4ftc per lb for 60-70's Of the new
crop in bags The European demand for
Prunes promises to be sharp this year, ..wing
to the partial failure of the French and Servian
crops. The California crop will be less than a
full crop, but will run to large sizes, and from
present Indications the Prune growers will real
ize large returns -this year.
Sale of new Nonpareil Almonds at 10c.
There is a very fair demand for new Honey,
which is not coming in very freely as yet
All new fruits are firm and in demand, with
a decided upward tendency. This will be a j
great dried fruit year. ... .

HIDES, TALLOW, WOOL AND HOPS.
Hides and Hops show no new features. The
latter are In demand, but growers decline to
contract at current bids.
. A local house says of Wool: "There Is noth
ing specially new to report in our local Wool
market. Choice Northern Wool is coming for
ward and finds ready sale at full figures. Most
of the Wool from the middle counties, as well
as from th- coast and the San Joaquin Val- ;
lev, has been marketed. Oregon Is sending i
down a liberal share of Its Wool clip, whicn
is being bandied by scourers. A limited quan- !
tity of Valley Oregon is being received, but j
Just yet these coarse Wools are not In demand. :
but we look for a good market for them In the j
fall. A few parcels of Southern Coast Lambs'
Wool have been received, but they do not at- I
tract attention. On the whole the outlook for
Wool Is as good as could be wished for."

LEATHERâ A local circular says: "Some
lines of Leather have been comparatively quiet,
but the general leather trade can be quoted as
entirely satisfactory. It may be said that there
is too small a margin between the raw material
and the finished product, and the tanners are
now laying in stocks of very high-priced hides,
which will show a loss when any reaction in
the market occurs. The real market has been
in harness and skirting and heavy sole Leather.
Large quantities of harness and skirting are
being shipped to the West and Southwest,
where California Leather, owing to its superior
quality finds a ready sale at fair prices. Sole
Leather has been doing fairly well." Sole, heavy,

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day. in the order of occurrence as to time. The
'second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
I and the last or right hand column gives the
; last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference ip the mean of the lower low waters.

SECRET CONTRACT IS
BROUGHT TO LIGHT
Traffic in Dead Bodies the Basis of
a Financial Deal
Coroner Mehrmann Threatened to Smash James Mc-
Carthy's Head if He Ever Divulged Any of
the Morgu. Location Scandal.
OAKLAND, Nov. 28, 1898. 1t is hereby covenanted and
agreed between H. B. Mehrmann, the party of the first
part, and the firm of Blair, McCarthy & Clark, the party
of the second part, that in consideration of value by the party of
the first part, he hereby covenants and agrees to establish the
Morgue at the quarters of the party of the second part for the
whole four years of his term of office as Coroner.
And the party of the second part does hereby agree and cov
enant to provide a suitable Morgue, to fit up and furnish an office
for the Coroner, to provide a suitable place where inquests shall be
held and to furnish and pay a competent deputy for said Coroner.
And it is further covenanted and agreed that should any un
foreseen accident or death occur to the party of the first part, that
his estate or assigns shall pay to the party of the second part one
hundred and four dollars for each and every month of the term
said Coroner would have been In office. Said one hundred and
four dollars per month is only to be made payable in case said Cor
oner's office shall be taken away from the place provided by said
party of the second part. (Signed)
h. b. mehrmann.
James McCarthy,
J. BLAIR,
W. S. CLARK.
Extract from a political contract.
Oakland Oflice San Francisco Call,
SOS Broadway, July 18.
The secret deal leading up to the un
seemly row between the Supervisors and
Coroner Mehrmann has been laid bare. It
shows one of the most remarkable
schemes to profit by the county's mu
tilated dead that was ever brought into |
public notice. Not till to-day was it
known how a public offlce and the emol
uments and perquisites can be bartered
and sold, the price, of course, always
having to be paid from the public treas
ury.
It has long been known that there was
a dark secret story behind all the political
trouble in the county government. Yes
terday Dr. Mehrmann showed the animus
existing on both sides, and to-day every
body felt free to talk and took advantage
of the freedom.
Three weeks after Coroner Mehrmann's
election Messrs. Blair and Clark, two Sac
ramento undertakers, appeared in Oak
land and gave out that they had money to
Invest ln business. They approached
James McCarthy, a local undertaker, and
showed him what purported to be a con
tract between them and Dr. Mehrmann
setting forth that In consideration of $5000
the Coroner-elect would establish the
Morgue where the two men decided to lo
cate in business. . irk and Blair con- .
suited with McCarthy, with the result that
It was agreed that the three should go ,
into business on ;i basis of JSOiX* as capital. i
McCarthy was to provide handsome quar
ters for the Morgue to represent his $6000
in the capital. He leased two stores, en
gaged a deputy, contracted for an ex
pensive dead wagon from the Bast cost
ing $800, and on January 1 was all pre
pared to enter, upon the Morgue business.
Messrs. Clark and Blair were to put Into
the business $3000 and were to be credited
with ?,'*""" as capital on the strength of the
deal they declared they had made with
Dr. Mehrmann. and which they gave as
surances was transacted in good faith at
a local bank. As New Year's day ap
proached (the time tiie Coroner was to
take office* Messrs. Blair and Clark were
not quite so positive thai they wanted to
ratify their partnership with McCarthy;
notwithstanding the fact that all their
advertisements .>. re out, that they had
caused the cards of I he firm to be placed
in the new directory and that the hand
some dead-wagon with "Blair, McCarthy
& Clark" was delivered In Oakland with
all its shining silver-plated trapping:.;
ready for the opening of ; .'- new Morgue.
Many conversations were held as to the
manner in which the $5000 which Clark
and Blair alleged they had paid Dr. Mehr
mann was to; come back to the firm. The
records of the dead for vera! years past
â were carefully gone over, and it was
agreed that in consideration of the growth
of Oakland and the certain increase of
morgue business owing to it being a rail
road town, the figures of Dr. Baldwin's
administration should be the basis of the
new deal in th* traffic of dead bodies.
Dr. Baldwin's figures showed that he
held 175, 185. 16! and 19.8 inquests during
his four ears In office. This was figured

OAKLAND GIRL WEDS
IN THE FROZEN NORTH
ALBERTA SWAN THE BRIDE OF
REV. A. E. HETHERINGTON.
Sacks of Gold Dust for Wedding Gifts
and Rice at 35 Cents Per
Pound Was Not Too Cost
ly to Throiv.
OAKLAND. July 18.â Particulars have
.-just been received by friends here of the
marriage of Miss 8. Alberta Swan of
Fruitvale to Rev. Albert E. Hetherington,
which nappy event took place at Dawson
City on June 20, Rev. Dr. Grant, a Pres
byterian minister, officiating, assisted by
Rev. James Warren.
Among the wedding presents, according
to the account, was a sack of gold dust
and a roll of greenbacks to the amount of
$235; The bride also received some very
pretty nuggets. A great throng saw the
bridal couple off on their wedding trip at
the wharf, and though rice costs 33 cents
a pound this indispensable feature was
not omitted.
The bride is the daughter of Mrs. 6. M.
Swat: formerly of High street. Fruitvale.
Last February sho accompanied her moth
er, brother and sister to Dawson City.
The groom is a graduate of the Manitoba
College of Toronto, Canada, and is of the
Methodist denomination.
Buggies and Bicycles Affected.
ALAMEDA, July 18.â The City Trustees
may try to compel every vehicle in town
to carry a lantern after dark. Bicyclists
have been forced to do this on nights
when the nioon did not shine. Recently
an effort "was made to force the wheel
men to carry lights every night in- the
year, and City Attorney Taylor was in
structed to draw Up an ordinance on
those lines. He offered it last night, but
with it was a written opinion that the
ordinance would not stand the test of law,
as It was plainly class legislation. The
Trustees concluded that they would make
every vehicle In the city flash a light
after the sun goes down, or let the wheel
men go as they are at present.

OAKLAND, July 18.â There is to be a

filed by Mrs. Eva M. Beagole, who

Beaver charges that Mrs. Beagole is

to have charge of her.

Chinese Slavekeeper Arrested.
Wong Quan, 12' 2 Spofford alley, was
arrested last night on a warrant charging
him with having conducted a house of Ill
fame after notice nad been served on him
to vacate. The arrest was made by Ser
geant Donovan, who swore to the com
plaint. Thirty days ago notice was served
on Quan and several other slavekeeperÃ
ordering them to close their houses, but
they disregarded the order and the arrest
was the result. The case will be a sort
of test and will be energetically pushed
by the vigilance committee recently or
ganized for that purpose.

VICTIM A PIONEER RESIDENT
OF ALAMEDA.
Wandered Away From Camp at
Boulder Creek and "Was Found
in a Dying Condition Two
Days Later.
*
ALAMEDA, July IS.â Mrs. Elizabeth
Ham of 2002 Pacific avenue, a lady past
the allotted three-score and ten, was lost
last Sunday night in the Santa Cruz
Mountains near Boulder Creek, and was
not found by the rescuing party until late
yesterday afternoon. She was .discovered
In a deplorable condition about four miles
from Boulder Creek and it -is feared she
has suffered so from exposure and fright
that she may not recover.
The aged lady has been camping near
Boulder Creek with her son-in-law and
daughter, Mr. and Mrs. John P. Forbes,
of 123 Albion avenue, San Francisco, and
Mrs. Thomas Payzer of Alameda. On
Sunday morning she decided to take a
walk into the hills and Insisted that she
might go alone. The other members of
the party consented only after she prom
is. not to go very far.
When the morning passed and Mrs.
Ham did not return to camp Mr. and Mrs.
Forbes became anxious for her welfare
and began a search. No trace of her
could be found. A searching party was
organized in the afternoon and a sys
tematic hunt was instituted. Fifty men
tramped the hills through the redwoods
all night with lanterns and torches, but
no trace of Mrs. Ham was found. It was
scarcely believed that she could have
wandered more than a mile from the
camp and the ground for that radius was
gone over and over again without result.
Mrs. Forbes, her daughter, was frantic
with fear for her mother's safety.
Late yesterday afternoon some of the
searchers found the lost woman in a
densely wooded canyon about four miles
from camp, She was lying under a clump
of underbrush, too exhausted and bewil
dered to give an account of herself. Sue
had to be carried back to Boulder Creek
on an improvised litter.
Mrs. Ham has since remained In a semi
conscious condition, unable to give an ac
count of her trying experience during the
time Â»he wandered in the mountains. The
exposure and fright which she suffered
were so great that her condition ls seri
ous and it is feared she may not recover.
Mrs Ham is a pioneer resident of Ala
meda. Her son, 'hail*-.- Ham. is* a teach
er in the Polytechnic High School, San
Francisco.
EMPTIED HIS GUN AT
TWO MIDNIGHT BURGLARS
ROY SMITH SURPRISED THEM
AT THEIR WORK.
One of the Intruders Supposed to Be
Badly Wounded While Making
His Escape.
The residence of Hoy Smith nt 765 Capp
street was entered at 1 o'clock yesterday
morning by two burglars, who gained ad
mission by raising the kitchen window.
The noise made by the intruders awoke
Smith, and on listening he came to the
conclusion that there were some parties
In the house who had no right to be there.
Jumping out of bed, he donned some
clothing and hurriedly seized his shotgun,
which was within convenient reach, and
proceeded to explore the premises. On
arriving at the kitchen he was confronted
by two men, who were at work packing
up such articles as they thought would
be useful to them in their line of business.
The burglars beat a hasty retreat
through the window, which tuey had left
open for that purpose, and Smith took
a shot at them as they went out. One
of the men gave a yell of pain and rolled
over several times cursing and swearing
rind saying that he was shot. An ex
amination of the fence and the ground
where the man fell showed that it was
covered with blood and that Smith's aim
was good. In the meantime, however,
the burglars got away.

CALIFORNIA TO HAVE A
NEW MEDICAL SOCIETY
California is to have a new medical so
ciety. This was definitely decided last
night at a meeting of a committee ap
pointed for. the purpose of considering the
; advisability of such action in the office
of Dr. D. a. Hodgehead. The members
of the committee are Its. C. F. Buckley,
W. F. MoNutt; S. F. Long. C, H. Rosen
thal. J. A. Miller, Winslow Anderson and
D. A. Hodgehead of San Francisco and
B. 7.. Henderson of Napa and E. W. King
of Ukiah, Mendocino County. All were
present at the meeting with the exception
of Dr. McNutt, who is traveling in Eu
rope. â¢ â .â â â â 'â â  S > â 
Or. Buckley presided and it was the
sense of the committee that a new society
should at once be formed, to be Incorpor
ated as soon as practicable, the name of
the society to be the California Medical
Association. It was also decldede that the
new society should conform to the code
of Ethics of the American Medical Soci
ety and Instead of issuing an annual vol
ume to issue a monthly publication, the
subscription to which, together with the
membership fee in the association to not
exceed $3 a year. It is proposed to hold
the first annual meeting in October next
and to meet In that month each succeed
ing year. â '"'--. _- yy.
The committee will report its conclu
sions in the matter to the San Francisco
Clinical Society, which is the instigator
of the movement, to-night and it is ex
pected that the necessary steps to carry
through the plan will be at once taken.
The movement to organize a new State
society of medicos is the result of a fac
tional quarrel in the ranks of the exist
ing organization, which has caused many
! members to withdraw. . -

The report shows 855 esses as coming

There were 705 autopsies and 443 Inquests.
The suicides numbered 158, or more than

139 Jews 9, Pagans 4, infidels and free
thinkers none, of unknown religion 6. The

United States 54, Germany 33, Ireland 16,
England 10, unascertained 18, China 4, It
aly 4, Canada 8, Denmark 8. The causes
of suicide were: Despondency 82,.tempor
ary Insanity 83, unknown causes 19. sick
ness 10, jealousy and domestic troubles 8.
There were 43 -murders during the year,
nearly at tho rato of one each week, and
one justifiable homicide. There were 187
accidental deaths, 878 from natural causes
and 89 from causes unknown.^ ' *

ical analyses in cases of suspected poison
ing and $500 for the recovery of drowned

Many Growers Think It the Salvation
of the Wine Industry Call for
a Meeting at Healds
burg Issued,
*
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, July 18.â Santa Clara
County grape growers and wine mak
ers are almost unanimous in accepting
the proposition of H. J. Crocker, presi
dent of the Winemakers" Corporation,
to enter into contracts to buy the en
tire grape crop for the next seven years
at $14 per ton. Nearly all the viticul
turllsts have already signed cards
agreeing to this, and it is expected the
rest of them will do so in a few days.
At a meeting of growers and wine
men held in Champion Hall, this after
noon, they were unanimous in this re
spect. About seventy-five of the most
prominent vineyardists in the county
were present. E. S. Guppy presided as
chairman, and A. Barstow acted as sec
retary. Many thousands of acres of
vines were represented, although not
one-third of those favoring Crocker's
proposition were present. Among those
present were: D. M. Delmas, P. Sala
zar of Livermore, Peter Ball. John Sni
der, George B. McKee, J. D. Williams.
j Alex Montgomery, W. B. Rankin and R.
T. Pierce.
E. S. Guppy stated that the object of
1 the meeting was to obtain the senti
! ment of winemen and growers in regard
> to the offer of H. J. Crocker to enter
| into contract to buy grapes at $14 per
! ton for seven years. He said this was
seemingly a good price and would pay
12 per cent on a $250 valuation. He
I stated that Crocker would distribute
the product among the various winer
-1 ies. All will be benefited by selling
! their grapes at the figure named and it
! will enhance the value of vineyards
' from 30 to 40 per cent. This would
benefit growers greatly and enable
: them to get better advances on their
crops. _
It was stated that in Sonoma County
growers had contracted to sell their
grapes for $12 per ton before Crocker's
, offer was made and that Southern
California growers had contracted at
$11 and $I*. , a
D M. Delmas favored Crocker s offer.
11. "considered $14 cash per ton on deliv
ery was very good, much better than he
had received during the last seven or
eight years. J. H. Henry was of the
â same opinion. Other growers spoke
along the same lines. Mr. Guppy said
that Crocker's offer would result In
good to all the viticultural interest .if
the State. Grapes bought by Crocker
' will be delivered to the various winer
, ies in county for pressing and most
likely to the winery nearest the grower.
W. B. Rankin, a large wine maker
I and grower, favored the offer made by
Crocker and said it would not hurt
I wine men. Grapes for the last ten years
; have averaged $10 per ton. The vine
var.lists will not replant nor care for
their vineyards. At $14 per ton grow
ers 'an afford to plant resistant stock.
Something must be done to save the
vineyards. The wineries are a second
ary consideration, and If the vineyards
are not saved they will all be turned
Into barns.
Most of the growers of Alameda
County will favor Mr. Crocker's propo
sition. Meetings of growers to take
action will be held In about a week. ,
A letter was read from H. Bendel, a
i fruit packer and fruit grower, urging
! the grape growers and wine makers to
! sign the contract with Crocker. All
those present who had not yet signed
the agreement did so. The following
i resolution was unanimously adopted:
"Resolvedâ That we, the grape grow
ers and wine makers of Santa Clara
County, heartily approve of the propo
sition of Henry J. Crocker to buy our
grapes for the next seveji years at $14
I a ton. being fully convinced that the
j said proposition, if carried out, is the
! most advantageous that could be ad
. vanced for the interests of grape grow
> ers and wine makers of this county and
j of the entire State."
Committees have been appointed to
! canvass the various sections and see
â  that all wine men and growers sign.

LEONARD HAGY
WAS SHORT IN
HIS ACCOUNTS
Left Winchester Arms
Company.
â â
WAS A TRUSTED CASHIER
â â¦ â
MANAGER MULLER REFUSES TO
MAKE A STATEMENT.
â¦
Hagy Declares That He Is Not Short
the Amount Charged and That
Muller Has Given
Him Away.
â¢â â
Leonard A. Hagy, bookkeeper of the
Winchester Arms Company, is said- to be
$1100 short in his accounts to the com
pany and has been discharged. The
amount of the shortage is stated to have
been charged to the account of E. H.
Ladd. Rudolphe Muller, the manager of
the company, when called upon and asked
for a statement, refused to say anything,
except that Hagy had left the employ-,
ment of. the company. Beyond this he
would not give out anything in the way
of a statement. He said that he would
"not throw stones at a dog that was
down," but beyond this he would not
make any statement.
Hagy was seen last night by a Call cor
respondent. At tirst he was loth to dis
cuss his shortage, claiming that he knew
nothing whatever about it; but finally
concluded to give his side of the story.
"I admit," said he. "that there was a
little difference in the figures ot the Ladd
account, and that led to the severing of
mv connection with the Winchester Arms
Company. When I left, Mr. Muller the
manager, promised me that I would not
be punished and that the difficulty would
not be mad*, public. I do not think he has
kept faith with me.
"The eleven hundred dollar figure of the
Ladd account is not correct; the difference
does not come un to that amount. Re
garding the statement that 1 spent the
money at the horse races and coursing
matches 1 have nothing to say. Mr. Mul
ler promised me faithfully he would say
nothing about this affair, and 1 cannot
understand how it got out."
Hagy has been connected with the Win
chester Arms Company as bookkeeper and
cashier for several years. A few days
ago the manager. Mr. Muller, looked over
the ledger and found a balance of SHOO
charged to Ladd & Co. Muller visited
Ladd and requested a settlement. Ladd
laughed at the amount asked for and said
the balance he owed the Winchseter peo
pie did not exceed $40. Bookkeeper Hagy
was taxed With the discrepancy, and ad
mitted, so it is state*], that he had been
holding out small amount.- from the firm
for some months and charging them to
the Ladd account. .
Hagy is a prominent local Native Son,
being a past president of Halcyon Par
lor ' He was appointed by Chairman
Brick of the Board of City Trustees a
member of the Dewey monument commit
tee to represent the Native Sons. He was
i married about two years ago to Miss Eva
! Evans, who was an employe of the tele
j phone company. His home is at 925 Tay
lor avenue. Alameda.

WILL DISCUSS A
BOARD OF INQUIRY
It Is Expected That To-Day's Session
of the Peace Conference Will
Be Very Lively.
THE HAGUE, July 18.â The sub-com
mittee of the arbitration committee of the
Peace Conference met this afternoon to
draft the amendments demanded at yes
terday's session of the plenary committee
and to prepare for to-morrow's meeting,
when the discussion of tin question of an
international board of inquiry Is expected
to create a lively debate.
As the exact nature of the objections
made were not known no action was
taken.
It Is understood Captain Alfred T. Ma
han, United States navy, retired, one of
the American delegates, has decided to
withdraw his proposed amendments to the
scheme for adapting the Geneva conven
tion to naval wars, owing to objections
on the part of Great Britain and other
powers.

OUR BLANCHE IS NOT
TIM FRAWLEY'S BRIDE
ACTRESS DENIES THE SOFT IM
PEACHMENT.
Manager Attributes It All to Corson
Clarke's Dyspepsia, Which He
Fears Has Affected
His Mind.
There is no legal reason why Blanche
Bates should tack on the name of Fraw
ley to her signature.
She says she is not Mrs. Frawley, Fraw
ley Bays she la not Mrs. Frawley, and as
they nre the two people who are in the
best position to know their statements
should be accepted as final. Our Blanche
took the report of her marriage to her
manager in her own charming, sunny
way. If she was annoyed she did not
show it. only laughed and exclaimed,
"Ridiculous; absurd!'!
Frawley, on the contrary, was vexed at
the silly report; not on his own account,
but because of Miss Bates.
"It's annoying for a woman," said the
gallant manager, "to have a lying report
of that kind circulated about her. Corson
Clarke's aggravated stomach trouble has
probably gone to his head. That is the
only wav I can account for it."
Mies Bates explains the slander by
Clarke's desire to gel his name before
the public. "There is nothing he could
say of himself, so he stood sponsor for
lies about others," said Miss Bates.
So the story of the manager wedding his
star is not true, nor is there any possi
bility of it ever being true, for the man
ager declared: "1 shall never marry. Im
too much of a crank to make any woman
! happy. And 1 respect woman too highly
| to link her fate with mine."
SUITS WILL FOLLOW
THE NEWMAN WRECK
Teachers Employ Lawyers and will
Ask for Heavy Dam
ages.
LOS ANGELES, July IS.â II. E. Foster
of McConnellsville, Ohio, and Mrs. Jane
Glasgow of St. Louis are about to bring
suit against the Southern Paciflc Com
pany for injuries and other damages, the
result of th* accident which occurred at
Newman Station last week during the
run of the special St. Louis National Ed
ucational Association train to Los An
geles.
The firm of Montgomery, Earle &
Hutchison of this city is now preparing
the complaints in the two cases. Other
suits also will follow by those who are
invalided and now here. Mr. Foster will
demand $1000 from the railroad, while Mrs.
Glasgow will place her damages at $3000.
Death of Mrs. Frost.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY. July IS.â
Mrs. Laura Frost, mother of Mrs. George
E. Howard, died this afternoon at Stan
ford University of heart failure. De
ceased was on a visit to her daughter, at
whose home here there was a family re
union. Mrs. Frost's death was quite un
expected, though she has been an invalid
for some years. The remains will be sent
to Lincoln, Neb., for interment. Dr. and
Mrs. George E. Howard will accompany
the remains. Mrs. Frost leaves consider
able property, chiefly in the Frost addi
tion to the city of Lincoln, Neb.
Counterfeit Remedies.
The Hostetter Company of Pittsburg,
Ph., have filed a number of bills in the
United States Circuit Court in this dis
trict against a number of persons in this
city, Oakland and Sacramento, who have
been discovered by them in the illegal
business of making and selling a coun
terfeit article of the Hostetter's Stomach
Bitters. The principal attorney of the
company has been engaged for several
days in' obtaining service upon the de
fendants, and attending to such other
matters as are necessary in the beginning
of a crusade against this unlawful busi
ness The Hostetter Company are deter
mined to protect their interest on this
coast and are determined to run down
the last counterfeiter <5f their goods at
any cost.
