CATHOLICS ARE
FREE IN CHINA
Missionaries to Rank as
Mandarins.
â â¦ â
DECREE OF THE EMPRESS
â â
ANTI-CHRISTIAN RIOTS IN FO
KIEN PROVINCE.
*
Viceroy Liv Has Received Instruc
tions From the Empress to Pre
vent the Landing of Eu
ropean Troops.
â¦
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TACOMA. July 18.â The steamship
j Olympia brings news that the Empress
! has issued a decree recognizing the
' Roman' Catholic religion throughout
I China and according an official grade to
all Catholic missionaries, who thus
; rank as mandarins. The French pro
tectorate of Catholics in China Is also
recognized with all its privileges. This
recognition was secured as a result of
strong French demands made following
I the murder of French missionaries in
! Interior provinces. It is hoped that the
| edict will give missionaries greater pro
i tection than they have had heretofore. j
I Anti-Christian riots in Fokien province
have caused all the missionaries to flee
to Foochow. .Seven Chinese Christians
were murdered during the riots.
It was reported that Rev. Mr. Phil
lips, his wife and other English mis
sionaries were murdered, but latest dis
patches announce their arrival at Foo
chow. Much missionary property was
destroyed. The British gunboat -.Iger
ine has arrived at Foochow.
A terrible explosion occurred last
month at the'Hokoku coal mine, Japan,
in which 220 persons were killed. Over
170 bodies have been recovered. A
naked lamp carried into the mine
caused the explosion.
The plague still rages in Formosa.
Up to June 25 there had been 1776
deaths since January 1 in Northern
Formosa.
The Nanking native correspondent of
the Shanghai Daily News reports that
Viceroy Liv, at Nanking, has received
a sensational secret edict from the Em
press Dowager directing him to use
armed forces to prevent the landing of
European troops in the Yang-tse-
Kiang Valley. The edict reads: "In
the event of the landing of armed
forces of European powers within the
jurisdiction of the imperial commis
t slonershlp of Nanyang you are hereby
I granted the liberty to employ armed
| resistance thereto. There will be no
' necessity of waiting for further in
structions from the throne. You will
be held responsible should there be any
failure in obeying these instructions
and It shall be in your dutyto notify our
commands to the various viceroys,
governors and provincial commanders
in-chief of maritime and river prov
inces comprised in your jurisdiction and
to see to it that effective opposition be
given to the foreign aggressors. Lib
erty is also given to you to keep back
taxes destined for the imperial ex
chequer in the event of lack of funds
to prosecute the war. Obey this in *-ar
and trembling."
Viceroy Liu's jurisdiction extends
over all the Yang-tse and maritime
provinces as regards foreign affairs and
military and naval matters. The Japa
nese home minister will shortly issue a
departmental notification relating to
the control of foreign religions. The
propagation of Christianity has here
tofore not been interfered with, and no
â  ' objection has been made to the erec
tion of churches, but the forthcoming ;
j notification will contain a clause re
' quiring that in the future before a
' church is erected the consent of the
I authorities must be obtained. â¢ '
TAPPED A CASH
REGISTER AS A JOKE
Excuse Made by a Suisun Man When
v Caught in the Act by a
Hotel Clerk.
SUISUtNT, July 18.â H. Bryan, pro
prietor of the Arlington Hotel, swore to
a complaint to-day charring Vincent
Bailey, formerly one of the proprietors
of the Louvre restaurant in this place,
with burglary. Mr. Bryan claims that
Bailey took money from his cash register
and has as a witness Leonard Downer,
night clerk in the Arlington Hotel. The
theft was committed at 4 o'clock this
morning. Bailey admitted having taken
some money from the register, but claims
it was done as a joke on Mr. Downer,
who is quite young and a new man in the
place.
Mr. Downer could not see where the
joke came in. He said that the defendant
and James Gray came into the place
shortly after 2 o'clock and for nearly two
hours stood up against the bar drinking.
Finally Gray was seized with a thirst for
Champagne, and there being none in sight
went to the liquor-room with Downer to
get a bottle. He held a lighted candle
while the clerk got the wine, and just as
Downer was about to shut the door he
heard the ring of the bell of the register.
He ran to the barroom, which he reached
in time to see Bailey coming from behind
the bar. Going to the drawer, he found
that money had been taken and imme
diately accused Bailey of taking It. Real
izing that denial was futile, Bailey dis
gorged, at the same time saying it was
only a josh.
When Mr. Bryan arose this morning
the matter was laid before him, with the
result that he swore out the warrant.
Bailey was taken before Justice Hitch
cock "this evening and held to appear be
fore the Superior Court, with bonds fixed
at $500.
ORTMAN IS SUSTAINED.
Gas Company's Franchise Is Assess
able in Stockton.
STOCKTON, July IS. â The County
Board of Equalization sustained Assessor
Ortman's assessment of the franchise of
the Stockton Gas and Electric Company,
the valuation being 1 $98,788. The company
protested against the assessment on the
ground that it's local property had already
been valued at 41000 in the general assess
ment of the company's property at the
head offlces in San Francisco.
District Attorney Ashley, gave an opin
ion at the request of the board holding
that the franchise was assessable where
the company was actually doing business,
and the board upon the reading of the
same denied the petition for the striking
out of the assessment. With this action
of the board all hopes by other corpora
tions of beating Ortman's assessments
end, as the members of the county
board are men who will not recede from
a position once taken. The protests of the
national banks will be disposed of short
ly and it is believed that in every in
stance Ortman will be sustained. If any
cutting is done the responsibility must
rest with the State Board of Equalization.
ANTI-DEBRIS ASSOCIATION
Will Press Suits Against Various
Mining Companies.
SACRAMENTO, July 18.â executive
committee of the Anti-Debris Association
held a meeting- to-day in the Supervisors'
room with Supervisor Jenkins in the
chair. R. T. Devlin, attorney for the as
sociation, reported the status of the suits
brought by the county of Yuba against
the Kate Hayes Mining Company, the
county of Sutter against John Spaulding
and others, and the county of Yuba
against Patrick Calabel and others. Prep
i arations for the prosecution of these
cases were discussed and It was decided
to push them to final issue.
The condition of the hydraulic mines in
the American, Bear, Yuba and Feather
rivers was also discussed. Some mines
have closed down on account of the water
supply and others are still running. The
association announced its intention to
make a thorough investigation of all min
ing territory, for the purpose of closing
down all mines .working injuriously be
fore the close of the next mining season.
PEOPLE FLEE FROM
HUNGRY MOSQUITOES
Insects Cause Inhabitants of a Snake
River Town to Seek Safety in
the Mountains.
BOISE, Idaho, July 18.â According to a
story from American Falls, a Snake River
town, the people there are being driven
from their homes by mosquitoes. Swarms
of the pestiferous insects, rivaling in size
and persistency the product of New Jer
sey, make life there unbearable. People
are leaving for the mountains as fast as
possible in order to escape absolute tor
ture. Sunday an attempt was . made to
hold divine services, but the mosquitoes
broke up the meeting, the preacher and
congregation fleeing from the building
covered with white spots.
It is reported that a babe left alone for
a short time was almost devoured by the
mosquitoes, being so badly poisoned that
it may not live. Drugs have been tried in
vain, and the people have determined to
take to the mountains until the pests
leave. ____________________________________
OIL DEVELOPMENT
A Pipe Line to Be Con
structed to the San
Joaquin.
Colonel A. F. Haraszthy, â¢ who has
been frequently seen in Fresno during
the past few months, arrived in town
again last night. He comes in consulta
tion with Judge Crichton, County Sur
veyor Hoxie, Dr. Maupin, A. C. Vandi
veer, N. P. Justy and others regard
ing the development of oil land. Colonel
Haraszthy was attracted to Fresno
County about four months ago by the
news of the oil strikes in the Panoche
belt. After satisfying himself that the
reports could be verified he engaged the
services of two experts and for the past
three months and a half they have been
pospecting this district. Colonel Har
aszthy immediately started out by
bonding every piece of property that
could be found by slightest indication
to be oil bearing. After some 7000 acres
had been located the colonel and his
associates made a more thorough ex
amination of the various properties
and decided that 2160 acres of the 7000
located were positively oil bearing. The
colonel, together with the above men
tioned gentlemen and a few* others, in
corporated the Union Consolidated Oil
and Transportation Company, with a
capital stock of $1,000,000, with head
quarters in San Francisco.
It is the intention of the company to
begin boring immediately on three dif
ferent pieces of the property. A central
storage tank will be erected and a pipe
line six inches in diameter constructed
from that point to the head of naviga
tion on the San Joaquin River, about
opposite Dos Palos. At the end of the
pipe line large tanks will be erected, a
switch will be run from the Southern
Pacific Railroad to the tanks, and
wharves constructed along the river
bank. The company will thus be en
abled to have either water or rail trans
portation, as the circumstances may re
quire. River boats of light draught
with iron oil tanks will be constructed
and their product hauled by water to
Stockton, Sacramento, Oakland, San
Francisco and San Jose, at which places
their oil wagons will be filled and the
consumer supplied. â Fresno Repub
lican. __^__________ fr
WOODLAND SEWER BONDS.
WOODLAND, July IS.â Eight years ago
Woodland was bonded for the purpose of
constructing a sewer system, water works
and a municipal public building. These
bonds mature in 1911. The City Trustees,
by virtue of a law enacted by the last
Legislature, have made an application for
an extension of time, which will make the
last of the bonds mature in 1951. and the
outlook is fa able that the application
will be granted.
Cotton Industry in Mexico.
CITY OF MEXICO, July IS.â The Mexi
can Government's annual report on the
cotton Industry of this country for the
last fiscal year has just been issued. It
shows that there are twenty-five cotton
mills in Mexico which operate 491, 759
looms and 29 stamping machines, and
employ 23,731 laborers. These factories
consumed over thirty million pounds of
cotton during' the past twelve months.

PROOF GIVEN THAT CHEYNE

Four "Witnesses Swear That Corcoran
Was in the Town of Burke on
the Afternoon of the
Wardner Riots.
â¦
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WALLACE. Idaho, July IS.â The attor
j neys for the prosecution announced to
. day at noon that they had finished the
evidence against Corcoran. The- morning
i was devoted to proving the death of
Cheyne, which was testified to by Coroner
| France, who visited the wounded man at
Wardner and went with him to Spokane,
, where an operation was performed and
1 where he afterward died. The bullet had
I penetrated the' abdominal cavity and the
J murdered man had not one chance in
twenty for life after being shot.
"When court convened this afternoon At-
I torney Reddy asked leave to recall one of
I the State's witnesses for further cross
! examination. Permission was granted
| and the defense called Dr. Collins, who
! testified in the early part of the trial that
I he had been compelled to go to Wardner
: by masked men at the point of a rifle,
I who told him his professional services
might be needed.
The prosecution objected when witness
! was asked if he had talkeu with any per-
I son since giving his testimony in regard
i to identifying the defendant.
"What is the purpose of this?" asked
J the court.
"To show an, attempt at bribery.' re
| sponded Mr. Reddy.
The objection was overruled, and wit
j ness testified that the day after being on
I the witness stand he had received a tele
i phone message at his home in Burke in
forming him that Mr. Borah, one of the
State's attorneys, wanted to see him.
Witness came to Wallace and met Mr.
: Borah near the jail.
"Relate the conversation that occurred
; at that time." said Mr. Reddy.
"I was told that if I could or would
! identify- Paul Corcoran as being on that
train they had something better to offer
me than remaining in Burke."
Witness further said that no specific
â  sum was named and that he had declined
any offer or assistance predicated upon
' his identification of defendant and had
* not seen defendant on that day. On ex
; amination by Mr. Borah he .admitted that
he had testified falsely before the Coro
. ner's inquest when he denied having been
' on the train, but said that he had done
so to save his life, as he feared to return
to Burke should he identify any one. Mr.
Borah then took the stand and denied
having made such a proposition to wit
ness.
The defense called P. W. Foy. a resident
of Wardner, who was present when
Cheyne was shot. He contradicted Mrs.
: Sinclair, who said she was assisted in
! carrying Cheyne to a place of safety by
j a man wearing a white mask, whom she
recognized as defendant. Foy swore the
' man who assisted her wore a black mask
I and that it was closely drawn over vis
â  features and held in place by being fas
tened to both his hat and his coat. Three
i ladies living in Burke testified to having
: seen the defendant in Burke between the
I hours of 12 and I o'clock ar.d Matt Bautn
i partner, assayer for the Tiger-Poorman
I Mining Company, saw him about 3 o'cloc*
tin the afternoon. Corcoran could not
i have been at Burke and have gone to
! Wardner at the same time. The defense
I will probably not occupy more than two
i days. , m
TO EXTERMINATE
THE MAYA INDIANS
Mexican Government Has Found the
Tribe Altogether Too Trou
blesome.
TAMPICO, Mexico. July IS.â .The Mcxi
i can Government will soon begin a cam
paign of extermination against the rebel
lious Maya Indians in the State of Yuca
tan.
The Government schoolship Yucatan has
arrived here on its way to New Orleans,
: where it will meet several small gunboats
'â  that were recently purchased by the War
i Department and convoy them to Pro
i gre^o, where they will be manned and
i Start on an- expedition up the Honda and
1 Belize rivers with a view of reaching tne
I heart of the territory of the warring
tribe There is already a large force of
troops massed In Yucatan awaiting the
: co-operation of these river boats in a con
: certed attack on the Indians.

CORONER HILL READY
TO EXHUME THE BODY
Says He Will Hold an Autopsy on
Mrs, Looria if Relatives so Desire.
Lays the Matter Before Chief of Police Lees and
Delivers Himself of an Opinion on the
Action of Dr. McMurdo.
CORONER HILL stands ready to
exhume the body of Mrs. Fannie
Looria should any relative of the
dead woman make the demand
that he investigate the ugly ru
mors persistently circulated since her
sudden taking off. . '
The Coroner's first information of the
demise of Mrs. Looria and of the strange
action of Dr. John R. McMurdo in Issu
ing a certificate of the cause of death,
without having so much as seen the body,
was obtained hen he picked up yester
day's Call and read the story of the death
and interment, He at once went to the
records, where he found that no report
of the case had ever been forwarded to
his office. The narrative of the circum
stances attending the decease of Mrs.
Looria made it plain to Dr. Hill that, foul
play or no foul play, the matter was one
for investigation by himself. The fact,
too, that Dr. Margaret Mahoney, acting
as the law prescribes a physician should
act in such case, had written a note
which she instructed should be taken to
the Coroner, strengthened this belief.
Coroner Hill called at once upon Chief
of Police Lees and laid the case before
him. The two agreed that the action of
Dr. McMurdo In th... issuance of the death
certificate had been entirely unauthorized,
and if generally practiced would make
the way easy for crime and almost im
possible" for its detection. The fact, how
ever, that the body was in a cemetery
in San Mateo County and that consider
able expense would have to be incurred
to exhume it, determined the Chief an.l
the Coroner in the conclusion that no
steps should be taken toward disinter
ment, unless some relative should make
the demand.
Coroner Hill, not satisfied with stopping
at this point, determined to call Dr. Mc-
Murdo to task for the part he had taken
in facilitating the burial of Mrs. Looria.
The Coroner decided to write a letter to
the ex-assistant city physician concern
ing the case, inquiring particularly why
Dr. McMurdo had stopped the note to Dr.
Hill written by Dr. Mahoney. The Coro
ner was busy writlfig the letter when seen
by a reporter for The Call.
'-â¢1 can't understand." he said, "why Dr.

It is not likely that any demand will be
made for an autopsy. The only surviving
relatives of the dead woman who made
any objection to the hasty burial of .Mrs.
Looria are the members of the Samuels
family, at 1103 Howard street. As pub
lished in yesterday's Call, Mr. and Mrs.
Samuels both made objection to the in
terment of the aged woman until the
Monday following her demise. They gave
as a reason that the death had been so
â  sudden, although they both have said
since that other and graver suspicions had
been aroused.
These suspicions are not shared by
Abraham Leszynsky. sexton of the syna
gogue at Post and Taylor streets, who
: had charge of the interment.
"The h^sty burial .of Mrs. Looria." he
said yesterday, "was made on my advice.
' She died on a Saturday morning and it
was the intention to bury her on Monday.
Her relatives are orthodox Jews, who will
not allow their dead to be injected with
embalming fluid. The car lines running
to the cemeteries will not take a corps*
two days after death unless the body has
been injected, therefore I suggested that
burial take place one day earlier than in
tended and she was buried on Sunday.
Everything was done in strict compliance
with the law. and I certainly would not
| have countenanced anything unlawful in
the case. The Jewish religion forbids any
post-mortem, and the haste is excusable
on that point."
The Samuels still contend that it had been
the oft-repeated intention of Mrs. Looria
to make some provision In her will for
their son. their impression being that. he
would receive half of the hard-earned
hoard lying at interest in the Hibernia
Bank. The death of Mrs. Looria Intestate
precluding the possibility of the heirship
of young Samuels, it is hardly possible
! that they will take any steps to probe
' into the manner of the death of Mrs.
Looria, particularly when such action
i would do them no good.
With the Samuels practically out of
consideration, the courts will ha\\> to busy
themselves with the question of the dis
tribution of the dead woman's money. On
the face this apears an easy task, but it
may not be. Looria. the surviving hus
band, claims one-half of the money on the
theory that it was community property.
His stepdaughter as strongly maintains
that it was not community- property; that
her mother had more than that when she
married Looria, and that it belongs en
tirely to her and not a cent to the wid
ower.
At present the daughter appears to be
living amicably with the stepfather, and
she says she will continue with him, not
withstanding the proffers of shelter from
benevolent Hebrew women, who have
taken pity on the girl's condition. She is
sickly and undoubtedly not strong in the I
head", still she insists that she alone is en
titled to her mother's money. Looria. ac
cording to the girl's story, told without
any feeling of bitterness toward the step
father, is equally bent on getting half of
it.
"Two weeks after my mother's death."
said the girl. "Looria asked me to go to
a lawyer's with him and sign over half
the money. He said it was his as well as
mv mother'sâ that he had helped to earn
it.' I told him it wasn't.''
"She came over to my house after her
father had asked her to sign for the
money.'' said Mrs. A. Davis of 186 Ship
ley street, "and told me about it. I said
to her. 'Mary, don't you be in a hurry to
sign your name. Wait till the court set
tles everything; then, if you want to, you
can give him half.' "
"Then I went back to my stepfather."
said the girl, "and told him. I wouldn't
sign." .

VALLEY ROAD PROGRESS.
The Stockton Section to Be in Opera
tion Next January Officers
Elected.
The San Francisco and San Joaquin
Valley Railroad held its annual meeting
yesterday for the purpose of electing its
officers and directors for the ensuing year.
E. P. Ripley, president of the Santa Fe,
was elected to fill the same position in
I the Valley Road Company, <'lavs Spreck
! els retiring from all active and official re
! lation with tne undertaking, of which he
| was the moving power. Tite retirement
docs not mean that Mr. Spreckels has
â  severed all connection with the road. He
i still holds a large bonded interest, his re-
I tirement from the presidency merely
'â  being made for the purpose of obtaining
more time to devote to his other airs.
This was the only change made among the
official heads of the road, Captain A. H.
Payson being re-elected to the position
of "vice president and Alexander Mackie
as secretary.
The board of directors now consists of
the following gentlemen: E, P. Ripley, A.
11. Payson. J. D. Spreckels, W. G. Nevin,
, A. F. Walker. E. N. Gibbs, Victor Maro
; witz. R. S. Hays, S. W. Van Bicklen and
H. C. Bush.
Chief Engineer W. B. Storey presented
his report, dealing with the technicalities
; of the road and the progress made during
i the last year, all of which has been pub
| lished in The Call. The report shows that
i aside from a little more minor work to be
, done on the different tunnels all that re
! mains is the construction of the track on
the seventy-mile from Stockton to Point
; Richmond. This will give the road, its
entire completion to the point where it
connects with the ferry service to this
city. . ,
The new boat beiitg built for that serv
ice will not be completed until some time
i next year; but the mad will doubtless be
! in operation in Its entirety by January I.
and the Ocean Wave, which was brought
down from the north for that purpose,
; will be carrying Into San Francisco pas
sengers from the second transcontinental
; line to the metropolis of the Pacific.
With the approval of President Ripley,
a circular was issued making the agents
: at Oakland, Sacramento, San Jose, Stock
i ton, San Francisco and Fresno joint
I agents of the Santa Fe.

took place last night at the clubhouse.

S. Hawkins. T. T. Williams and Hugo
| Kelt, appointed to. confer with the rail
roads regarding arrangementse for the
"Night in Venice" to be held some time
In September, reported that the Southern
Paclflc and North Pacific Coast companies
; had expressed a willingness to assisf the
celebration as far as possible, guarantee
ing a rate that the committee etlmated
would Insure a profit to the fete funds of
$2100. Mr. Stetson of the narrow-gauge
i line running to Sausailto had promised to
rejfly on the subject in forty-eight hours
' and a favorable rate is expected from
him.
The partial report of the committee was
received with satisfaction and the meet
ing adjourned to Wednesday evening
when it is expected Mr. Stetson will have
been heard from definitely.

tor of a meat market at 330S

jl Tamer of the Mission,"
and the title is most worthily
bestowed, for Mr. Seeliger is as much
at home in a cage with a ferocious
cat as when trimming a roast for a
customer. In the rear of his place of
business Mr. Seeliger maintains a
small menagerie, the star of which is
a ten-months-old pure-bred African
lioness, weighing over 200 pounds and
apparently possessed of the temper of
a demon. Mr. Seeliger says in regard
to her vicious looks and treacherous
manner. that "it is only her way", when
in action; but "her way' makes the
flesh of the ordinary spectator creep
with apprehension, while his eyes un
consciously wander toward the back
door of the market, the only conceiv
able avenue of escape from the im
provised den. The "lion tamer" gave
a private exhibition of his power over
the beast yesterday for the benefit of
a reporter and an artist from The Call
and Ben Davis, the ex-Market In
spector.
The lioness occupies a large iron
cage at one side of the yard, and the
moment her visitors appeared she gave
ample evidence that she is not of the
dopy, sleepy kind so often seen at
public "zoos." She opened her huge
mouth and crouched as though about
to leap through the iron; bars, while
she snarled and growled in a threat
ening manner. The three visitors
showed signs of nervousness, but Mr.

The shooting affray in the grocery store j
of Jo*-. Lercari on San Bruno road, near
Fifteenth avenue south, late Monday
night is still concealed in a fog of mys
tery. The theory of th*- police points to
a personal feud with perhaps a financial
dispute at the back of it.
Lercarl formerly owned the vegetable
gardens on Guerrero, between Fifteenth
and Sixteenth streets, and some time
since sold it to Ravegno and Magini. Ler
cari claims that there is due him on the
deal $320 80, but Magini asserts that he
only owes $40. Detectives. Gibson and
Wren have been working on the case and
have gathered some facts from the. Ital
ians in the neighborhood and the general
opinion is that Magini owes the larger
sum. /.. .-.;;':.;*.'
On Monday night Ravegno, who now
lies on a couch in the city and County
Hospital with flesh wounds of no serious
character, telephoned to Dercari'a wife at
the grocery store that he would out
to the place at 8 o'clock, and that they
had better look out for Magini, ,as . he
meant trouble.. Ravegno went out to the
place and was talking with the six other
hangers-on, when the masked men en
tered and began the fusillade. -'..-'-
It is the belief that Magini and Lercari
have been playing . in one another's
hands In regard to the business deal, and
that a hot feud was brewing among all
concerned. When the attempted murder '
took place Lercari was the only cool and
self-possessed man in the place. The two !
masked men who carried the guns walked
away leisurely after the shooting, one sit
ting on the steps of the store and the
other on the fence opposite.
Robbery could hardly have been the mo
tive of the shooting, as there were eight
men in the store at the time when' the
shots were fired. The "Mafia" broke
loose, is what the police think, with a hint
or two of a woman at the bottom of it.
As yet no arrests have been made.
At the time the two bold highwaymen
Invaded the store, and after the despera
does had made known the object of their
visit by ordering all hands up and dis
charging their pistols at the men in the
store, there was one little heroine. Dora
Careghlno, among the crowd who did not
fall on her face, but, on the contrary,
stood her ground and called for the po
lice. This little heroine Is the sister-in
law of Lercari and is only 10 years of age.
She is as pretty and Interesting as she is
brave. She gave a clear account of all
that transpired from the time the robbers
entered until she and her married sister.
Mrs. Lercarl, went out by the side door,
where they .obtained a full view of one
of the men. while he sat on the fence op
posite the house. -. -7

light shone full on the man's face, thus
giving the two ladles opportunity to give a
thorough description of the man to De

Steamer Newsboy Was Run on a Rock
by Guesswork and Captain and
â  Mate Are on a Vacation.
j Captains Bolles and Bulger, United
States local inspectors of steam vessels,
rendered a decision yesterday suspending
for four months the licenses of Captain
C. Ellefsen. master, and H. Hanson, chief I
mate, of- the coasting steamer Newsboy.
The Newsboy started from San â '. Fran- I
cisco for a northern port, and was run
upon a rock and damaged to the extent of
1600.
After announcing the written opinion I
Captain Bulger remarked that if masters
of coasting vesels desired to run their
steamers over the country roads In com
petition with automobiles' they should
take out a license for navigating the' land. I
Supervising inspector Bermingham . re
marked that if the careless mariners !
would take half as much pains to .keep |
their vessels from running upon the rocks
as they did to explain how they got there, !
there would be no shipwrecks of coastwise i
vessels. . â¢â  â  .-'â  â â¢ "â¢-.â

WILL JOIN MILLEB.
Edwin Stevens of the Tivoli Will
Soon Transfer His Affections
to the Columbia.
Edwin Stevens of the Tivoli will join
the Henry Miller Company at the close
of his present engagement. The matter
was quietly arranged between Miller and
Stevens on Monday, and the latter will
leave the Tivoli on the last of this month.
When Miller presents "Hamlet" at the
Columbia Stevens is to play the part of
the King. He is thoroughly familiar with
the part, having played every part in
"Hamlet."
amiability was not appreciated by
those whom he addressed, his assertion
was not disputed. Mr. Seeliger then
put the bear through a few tricks, but
the hairy-coated animal was playful
and harmless compared with his ill
looking neighbor.
Mr. Seeliger is a native of Dresden,
Germany, 39 years old. His parents
died when he was young and he learned
the trade of a butcher. He traveled
with Rentz's Circus, as celebrated in
Europe as is Barnum's In this country,
for two years, and it was during this
time that he acquired a love for fon
dling wild beasts. Twenty years ago
he came to this country and eleven
years ago settled in the Mission, near
Irs present location.
The lioness is a half-sister of Wal
lace, the big African lion at the
Chutes, and was purchased in Omaha
three months ago. Mr. Seeliger has
arranged to exhibit her at the Chutes,
probably next Sunday, but his primary
object in securing her is to go into the
business of raising lions. The bear
was brought from Siberia by a sea
captain friend of his. Mr. Seeliger
raises a bear or two every year for
the market, killing them about Christ
mas time, when there is a big demand
for bear meat. He says he intends to
put the bear in the cage with the
lioness soon and teach them to act
together. Mr. Seeliger is a good-look
ing, good-natured gentleman, as un
like the typical animal trainer as it is
possible to imagine; but there is no
questioning the Quality of his nerve.
DODGE MAKES
MORE CHANGES
IN HIS OFFICE
Five New Deputies
Are Appointed.
VICTIMS FOR THE BASKET
â * â
FAT BILL ALMON AND CHARLIE
FAY GIVEN BERTHS.
â¦ââ
When the Doctor Returns From His
Vacation It Is Expected That
Many Heads Will
Fall.
*
Conditions in Assessor Dodge's office are
sadly disturbed and the fortunate poli
ticians who have been holding down snug
berths in the offlce have been on the
anxious seat for the past week. Several
important changes in the running force
of the Assessor's office t were made yester
day and those who profess to know the
inside of the Assizor's political affairs
are authority for the statement that many
surprising changes will be made as soon
as Dr. Dodge returns from the country,
whither he went yesterday for a two
weeks' hunting trip.
Several heads are slated for the basket,
but just who the unfortunate will be is
not known. Five new deputies were ap
pointed yesterday by Dr. Dodge before he
left the offlce, and, judging from the
political connections of the new ap
pointees who will feed at the public crib
until the end of the next campaign, it
might be said that Dr. Dodge is attempt
ing to pour oil on the troubled waters of
Democracy and recognize certain elements
â¢of the party that have thus far been
slighted in the distribution of patronage.
John Fitzgerald, who was Sheriff
Whelan's right bower during his incum
bency, has. been employed in Assessor
Dodge's office since Dodge assumed his
official duties, but "Jack," as he is fa
miliarly called in political circles, is now
out of a job, and Phil Fay, a brother of
Charlie Kay. Mayor Phelan's close-up ad
visor, took Fitzgerald's place on the pay
roil. "Bill" Almon, whose political im
portance is due to his strange resemblance
to Sam Rainey and the fact that he is
the lauded leader of affairs political in
the Thirty-fourth District, was given a
snug berth in the offlce. George Van
divier. Charlie Pratt and Al Murphy, well
known pollies, were also appointed.
As soon as Dr. Dodge returns it is ex
pected that more appointments will be
made and the official heads of a corre
sponding number of clerks and deputies
will be decapitated.
WILL PBESENT THE MEDALS.
Mechanics' Institute Will Give Up
the Pavilion to the Native
Sons and Daughters.
At an Informal meeting of the board of
directors of the Mechanics' Institute,
which was held last night, it was decided
that the Mechanics' Institute shall be
turned over to the Native Sons and
Daughters whenever they shall desire for
the presentation of the medals which are
to be given to the First California Regi
ment. Douglass Young, the editor of
Freedom, published at Manila, also ap
peared at the meeting and stated to me
members that he personally knew that the
Filipinos were in favor of making friends
with the people of the United States and
that he thought that a great good could
be accomplished by the fair if the Fili
pino exhibit were given a good show.

MRS. LOUISA KOPP
HELD FOR MANSLAUGHTER
JUDGE CONLAN DECIDES THERE |
WAS NO PREMEDITATION.
The Woman Is in the Last Stages of
Consumption and Had Almost
to Be Carried to and
From Court.
Mrs. Louisa Kopp was yesterday held
to answer before the Superior Court by
Judge Conlan on the charge of man
slaughter for shooting and killing her
husband. Charles Kopp, on June 28 last.
Her bonds were fixed in $1000. .
The Judge in rendering his decision said ;
that both sides agreed upon the facts as
to the shooting, but the question arose
as to whether it was a case of justifiable j
homicide. While morally there was a
great deal of merit in the part enacted
by the defendant, still it had been shown
that her husband came to his death at
her hands throne^, violence and through
the medium of a deadly weapon. The
Judge said that while sympathy was with
the defendant, he would not assume the
responsibility of discharging her. He
could not give way to his feelings, but
had to deal with the facts.
"While I must conclude, continued the 1
Judge "that a crime has been committed,
I do not believe that it is a case of homi- I
cide- and altnough legally there was no
justification for it. there was no premed
itation and it could be nothing more than
manslaughter. I have even grave doubts
as to its being that. If I were a juror
on the case. I could readily realize what
my verdict would be; but as a commit
ting magistrate, I shall hold the defend
ant to answer on the charge of man
slaughter and will fix her hail at the
lowest sum ever set in the courts on such
a charge, viz.. $1000. *'
The defendant had almost to be car
ried to and from the court by Bailiff
Kelly and Officer Bella as she is in the .
last stages of consumption. Her attor
ney. ex-Judge Ferral. will have her out
on* bonds to-day. 'â 
. â  m â 
Death of Ralph Gwin.
Ralph Gwin. the 18-year-old son of Wil
liam Gwin. the United States Shipping
Commissioner at this port, died last Mon
day at the family residence, corner of
Franklin street and Broadway, after an
illness of several weeks. After a course
in Trlnitv School in this city he studied

A Bold Soy Robber.
Mrs. Frankenau, 2100 Van Ness avenue,
reported to the police yesterday that while
riding on a Kearny-street car on Monday
afternoon a boy jumped on the car be
tween Post and Sutter streets and
snatched her purse, containing from $7 to
$10 -The boy was about 12 years of age
and shabbily dressed in a brown coat,
light trousers and gray hat. Detective
Graham was detailed on the case. .
